codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="230800" data-framerate="12" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv/Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv.240p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="232520" data-framerate="12" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv/Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="422256" data-framerate="12" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="540" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv/Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv.360p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="459488" data-framerate="12" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="540" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv/Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv.360p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This video was taken by the crew of <a href="/wiki/Expedition_29" title="Expedition 29">Expedition 29</a> on board the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">ISS</a>. The pass starts from just northeast of the island of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> over the North Atlantic Ocean to central Africa, over <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Atlantic Ocean</b> is the second-largest of the world's five <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, with an area of about 106,460,000 km<sup>2</sup> (41,100,000 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA-how-big_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-how-big-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic_Ocean_–_Britannica_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic_Ocean_–_Britannica-3">[3]</a></sup> It covers approximately 20% of <a href="/wiki/Earth#Surface" title="Earth">Earth's surface</a> and about 29% of its water surface area. It is known to separate the "<a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>" of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> from the "<a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>" of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> in the European perception of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">the World</a>.
</p><p>The Atlantic Ocean occupies an elongated, S-shaped basin extending longitudinally between <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> to the east, and the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> to the west. As one component of the interconnected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a>, it is connected in the north to the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> in the southwest, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> in the southeast, and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> in the south (other definitions describe the Atlantic as extending southward to <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>). The Atlantic Ocean is divided in two parts, by the <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Counter_Current" title="Equatorial Counter Current">Equatorial Counter Current</a>, with the <b>North(ern) Atlantic Ocean</b> and the <b>South(ern) Atlantic Ocean</b> split at about <a href="/wiki/8th_parallel_north" title="8th parallel north">8°N</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientific explorations of the Atlantic include the <a href="/wiki/Challenger_expedition" title="Challenger expedition"><i>Challenger</i> expedition</a>, the <a href="/wiki/German_Meteor_expedition" title="German Meteor expedition">German <i>Meteor</i> expedition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Lamont%E2%80%93Doherty_Earth_Observatory" title="Lamont–Doherty Earth Observatory">Lamont–Doherty Earth Observatory</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Hydrographic_Office" title="United States Navy Hydrographic Office">United States Navy Hydrographic Office</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">Toponymy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Toponymy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1745" data-file-width="2528" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg/220px-1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg/330px-1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg/440px-1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aethiopian_Ocean" title="Aethiopian Ocean">Aethiopian Ocean</a> in a 1710 French map of Africa</div></div></div>
<p>The oldest known mentions of an "Atlantic" sea come from <a href="/wiki/Stesichorus" title="Stesichorus">Stesichorus</a> around mid-sixth century BC (Sch. A. R. 1. 211):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MangasPlácido1998_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-MangasPlácido1998-7">[7]</a></sup> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">Atlantikôi pelágei</i></span> (Greek: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ἀτλαντικῷ πελάγει</span></span>; English: 'the Atlantic sea'; etym. 'Sea of <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(mythology)" title="Atlas (mythology)">Atlas</a>') and in <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">The Histories</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> around 450 BC (Hdt. 1.202.4): <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">Atlantis thalassa</i></span> (Greek: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ἀτλαντὶς θάλασσα</span></span>; English: 'Sea of Atlas' or 'the Atlantic sea'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>) where the name refers to "the sea beyond the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Heracles" title="Pillars of Heracles">pillars of Heracles</a>" which is said to be part of the sea that surrounds all land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In these uses, the name refers to <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(mythology)" title="Atlas (mythology)">Atlas</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, who supported the heavens and who later appeared as a frontispiece in Medieval maps and also lent his name to modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlas_(geography)" title="Atlas (geography)">atlases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford-Dict_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-Dict-10">[10]</a></sup> On the other hand, to early <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> sailors and in Ancient Greek mythological literature such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>, this all-encompassing ocean was instead known as <a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Oceanus</a>, the gigantic river that encircled the world; in contrast to the enclosed seas well known to the Greeks: the Mediterranean and the Black Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In contrast, the term "Atlantic" originally referred specifically to the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> in Morocco and the sea off the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> and the North African coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford-Dict_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-Dict-10">[10]</a></sup> The Greek word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">thalassa</i></span> has been reused by scientists for the huge <a href="/wiki/Panthalassa" title="Panthalassa">Panthalassa</a> ocean that surrounded the supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> hundreds of millions of years ago.
</p><p>The term "<b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aethiopian_Ocean" title="Aethiopian Ocean">Aethiopian Ocean</a></b>", derived from <a href="/wiki/Aethiopia" title="Aethiopia">Ancient Ethiopia</a>, was applied to the Southern Atlantic as late as the mid-19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmCyclo-1873_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmCyclo-1873-12">[12]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>, the Atlantic was also known to English cartographers as the <b>Great Western Ocean</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steele1986_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steele1986-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>The pond</b> is a term often used by British and American speakers in reference to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Atlantic_Ocean" title="Northern Atlantic Ocean">Northern Atlantic Ocean</a>, as a form of <a href="/wiki/Meiosis_(figure_of_speech)" title="Meiosis (figure of speech)">meiosis</a>, or ironic understatement. It is used mostly when referring to events or circumstances "on this side of the pond" or "on the other side of the pond", rather than to discuss the ocean itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The term dates to 1640, first appearing in print in pamphlet released during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>, and reproduced in 1869 in <a href="/wiki/Nehemiah_Wallington" title="Nehemiah Wallington">Nehemiah Wallington</a>'s <i>Historical Notices of Events Occurring Chiefly in The Reign of Charles I</i>, where "great Pond" is used in reference to the Atlantic Ocean by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Windebank" title="Francis Windebank">Francis Windebank</a>, Charles I's <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_(England)" title="Secretary of State (England)">Secretary of State</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent_and_data">Extent and data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Extent and data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For complete lists of marginal seas and borders of the Atlantic, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_seas#Atlantic_Ocean" title="List of seas">List of seas § Atlantic Ocean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_the_oceans#Atlantic_Ocean" title="Borders of the oceans">Borders of the oceans § Atlantic Ocean</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the extent of the Southern Ocean, see <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean#Definitions_and_use" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean § Definitions and use</a>.</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a> (IHO) defined the limits of the oceans and seas in 1953,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO-1953_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-1953-18">[18]</a></sup> but some of these definitions have been revised since then and some are not used by various authorities, institutions, and countries, see for example the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a>. Correspondingly, the extent and number of oceans and seas vary.
</p><p>The Atlantic Ocean is bounded on the west by North and South America. It connects to the Arctic Ocean through the <a href="/wiki/Denmark_Strait" title="Denmark Strait">Denmark Strait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland_Sea" title="Greenland Sea">Greenland Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a>. To the east, the boundaries of the ocean proper are Europe: the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> (where it connects with the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>—one of its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginal_sea" title="Marginal sea">marginal seas</a>—and, in turn, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, both of which also touch upon Asia) and Africa.
</p><p>In the southeast, the Atlantic merges into the Indian Ocean. The <a href="/wiki/20th_meridian_east" title="20th meridian east">20° East meridian</a>, running south from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Agulhas" title="Cape Agulhas">Cape Agulhas</a> to <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> defines its border. In the 1953 definition it extends south to Antarctica, while in later maps it is bounded at the <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60° parallel</a> by the Southern Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO-1953_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-1953-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Atlantic has irregular coasts indented by numerous bays, gulfs and seas. These include the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Davis_Strait" title="Davis Strait">Davis Strait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark_Strait" title="Denmark Strait">Denmark Strait</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian Sea</a>, almost all of the <a href="/wiki/Scotia_Sea" title="Scotia Sea">Scotia Sea</a>, and other tributary water bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA-World_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-World-1">[1]</a></sup> Including these marginal seas the coast line of the Atlantic measures 111,866 km (69,510 mi) compared to 135,663 km (84,297 mi) for the Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA-World_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-World-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Including its marginal seas, the Atlantic covers an area of 106,460,000 km<sup>2</sup> (41,100,000 sq mi) or 23.5% of the global ocean and has a volume of 310,410,900 km<sup>3</sup> (74,471,500 cu mi) or 23.3% of the total volume of the earth's oceans. Excluding its marginal seas, the Atlantic covers 81,760,000 km<sup>2</sup> (31,570,000 sq mi) and has a volume of 305,811,900 km<sup>3</sup> (73,368,200 cu mi). The North Atlantic covers 41,490,000 km<sup>2</sup> (16,020,000 sq mi) (11.5%) and the South Atlantic 40,270,000 km<sup>2</sup> (15,550,000 sq mi) (11.1%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ETOPO1_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-ETOPO1-4">[4]</a></sup> The average depth is 3,646 m (11,962 ft) and the maximum depth, the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Deep" title="Milwaukee Deep">Milwaukee Deep</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Trench" title="Puerto Rico Trench">Puerto Rico Trench</a>, is 8,376 m (27,480 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biggest_seas_in_Atlantic_Ocean">Biggest seas in Atlantic Ocean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Biggest seas in Atlantic Ocean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bathymetry">Bathymetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Bathymetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1825" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg/220px-Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg/330px-Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg/440px-Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/False_color" title="False color">False color</a> map of ocean depth in the Atlantic basin</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a> of the Atlantic is dominated by a <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">submarine mountain range</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</a> (MAR). It runs from 87°N or 300 km (190 mi) south of the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> to the subantarctic <a href="/wiki/Bouvet_Island" title="Bouvet Island">Bouvet Island</a> at <a href="/wiki/54th_parallel_south" title="54th parallel south">54°S</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHC-MAR_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-MAR-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-Atlantic_Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mid-Atlantic Ridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</a></div>
<p>The MAR divides the Atlantic longitudinally into two halves, in each of which a series of basins are delimited by secondary, transverse ridges. The MAR reaches above 2,000 m (6,600 ft) along most of its length, but is interrupted by larger transform faults at two places: the <a href="/wiki/Romanche_Trench" title="Romanche Trench">Romanche Trench</a> near the Equator and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gibbs_Fracture_Zone" title="Gibbs Fracture Zone">Gibbs Fracture Zone</a> at <a href="/wiki/53rd_parallel_north" title="53rd parallel north">53°N</a>. The MAR is a barrier for bottom water, but at these two transform faults deep water currents can pass from one side to the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LevGood-2003_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-LevGood-2003-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The MAR rises 2–3 km (1.2–1.9 mi) above the surrounding ocean floor and its <a href="/wiki/Rift_valley" title="Rift valley">rift valley</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Divergent_boundary" title="Divergent boundary">divergent boundary</a> between the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian</a> plates in the North Atlantic and the <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African</a> plates in the South Atlantic. The MAR produces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basaltic_lava" title="Basaltic lava">basaltic volcanoes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull" title="Eyjafjallajökull">Eyjafjallajökull</a>, Iceland, and <a href="/wiki/Pillow_lava" title="Pillow lava">pillow lava</a> on the ocean floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The depth of water at the apex of the ridge is less than 2,700 m (1,500 <a href="/wiki/Fathom" title="Fathom">fathoms</a>; 8,900 <a href="/wiki/Foot_(unit)" title="Foot (unit)">ft</a>) in most places, while the bottom of the ridge is three times as deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The MAR is intersected by two perpendicular ridges: the <a href="/wiki/Azores%E2%80%93Gibraltar_Transform_Fault" title="Azores–Gibraltar Transform Fault">Azores–Gibraltar Transform Fault</a>, the boundary between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nubian_Plate" title="Nubian Plate">Nubian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian plates</a>, intersects the MAR at the <a href="/wiki/Azores_Triple_Junction" title="Azores Triple Junction">Azores Triple Junction</a>, on either side of the Azores microplate, near the <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_north" title="40th parallel north">40°N</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> A much vaguer, nameless boundary, between the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American</a> plates, intersects the MAR near or just north of the <a href="/wiki/Fifteen-Twenty_Fracture_Zone" title="Fifteen-Twenty Fracture Zone">Fifteen-Twenty Fracture Zone</a>, approximately at <a href="/wiki/16th_parallel_north" title="16th parallel north">16°N</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1870s, the <a href="/wiki/Challenger_expedition" title="Challenger expedition"><i>Challenger</i> expedition</a> discovered parts of what is now known as the Mid-Atlantic Ridge, or:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>An elevated ridge rising to an average height of about 1,900 fathoms [3,500 m; 11,400 ft] below the surface traverses the basins of the North and South Atlantic in a meridianal direction from Cape Farewell, probably its far south at least as Gough Island, following roughly the outlines of the coasts of the Old and the New Worlds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> The remainder of the ridge was discovered in the 1920s by the <a href="/wiki/German_Meteor_expedition" title="German Meteor expedition">German <i>Meteor</i> expedition</a> using echo-sounding equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The exploration of the MAR in the 1950s led to the general acceptance of <a href="/wiki/Seafloor_spreading" title="Seafloor spreading">seafloor spreading</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHC-MAR_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-MAR-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the MAR runs under water but where it reaches the surfaces it has produced volcanic islands. While nine of these have collectively been nominated a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> for their geological value, four of them are considered of "Outstanding Universal Value" based on their cultural and natural criteria: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%9Eingvellir_National_Park" title="Þingvellir National Park">Þingvellir</a>, Iceland; <a href="/wiki/Landscape_of_the_Pico_Island_Vineyard_Culture" title="Landscape of the Pico Island Vineyard Culture">Landscape of the Pico Island Vineyard Culture</a>, Portugal; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gough_and_Inaccessible_Islands" title="Gough and Inaccessible Islands">Gough and Inaccessible Islands</a>, United Kingdom; and Brazilian Atlantic Islands: <a href="/wiki/Fernando_de_Noronha" title="Fernando de Noronha">Fernando de Noronha</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atol_das_Rocas" title="Atol das Rocas">Atol das Rocas</a> Reserves, Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHC-MAR_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-MAR-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_floor">Ocean floor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ocean floor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a list of fracture zones in the Atlantic, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_fracture_zones#Atlantic_Ocean" title="List of fracture zones">List of fracture zones § Atlantic Ocean</a>.</div>
<p>Continental shelves in the Atlantic are wide off Newfoundland, southernmost South America, and north-eastern Europe.
In the western Atlantic <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_platform" title="Carbonate platform">carbonate platforms</a> dominate large areas, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Blake_Plateau" title="Blake Plateau">Blake Plateau</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bermuda_Rise&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bermuda Rise (page does not exist)">Bermuda Rise</a>.
The Atlantic is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Passive_margin" title="Passive margin">passive margins</a> except at a few locations where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_margin" title="Active margin">active margins</a> form deep <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">trenches</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Trench" title="Puerto Rico Trench">Puerto Rico Trench</a> (8,376 m or 27,480 ft maximum depth) in the western Atlantic and <a href="/wiki/South_Sandwich_Trench" title="South Sandwich Trench">South Sandwich Trench</a> (8,264 m or 27,113 ft) in the South Atlantic. There are numerous submarine canyons off north-eastern North America, western Europe, and north-western Africa. Some of these canyons extend along the continental rises and farther into the abyssal plains as deep-sea channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LevGood-2003_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-LevGood-2003-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1922, a historic moment in cartography and oceanography occurred. The USS <i>Stewart</i> used a Navy Sonic Depth Finder to draw a continuous map across the bed of the Atlantic. This involved little guesswork because the idea of sonar is straightforward with pulses being sent from the vessel, which bounce off the ocean floor, then return to the vessel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the_Atlantic_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-the_Atlantic-33">[33]</a></sup> The deep ocean floor is thought to be fairly flat with occasional deeps, <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">trenches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seamount" title="Seamount">seamounts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_basin" title="Oceanic basin">basins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_plateau" title="Oceanic plateau">plateaus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Submarine_canyon" title="Submarine canyon">canyons</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Guyot" title="Guyot">guyots</a>. Various shelves along the margins of the continents constitute about 11% of the bottom topography with few deep channels cut across the continental rise.
</p><p>The mean depth between <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_north" title="60th parallel north">60°N</a> and <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60°S</a> is 3,730 m (12,240 ft), or close to the average for the global ocean, with a modal depth between 4,000 and 5,000 m (13,000 and 16,000 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LevGood-2003_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-LevGood-2003-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the South Atlantic the <a href="/wiki/Walvis_Ridge" title="Walvis Ridge">Walvis Ridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_Rise" title="Rio Grande Rise">Rio Grande Rise</a> form barriers to ocean currents.
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laurentian_Abyss" title="Laurentian Abyss">Laurentian Abyss</a> is found off the eastern coast of Canada.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_characteristics">Water characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Water characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg"><img alt="Visualisation of the Gulf Stream stretching from the Gulf of Mexico to Western Europe" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="6840" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg/400px-Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg/600px-Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg/800px-Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As the Gulf Stream meanders across the North Atlantic from the North American east coast to Western Europe its temperature drops by 20 °C (36 °F).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Circulacion_termohalina.jpg"><img alt="Map displaying a looping line with arrows indicating that water flows eastward in the far Southern Ocean, angling northeast of Australia, turning sough-after passing Alaska, then crossing the mid-Pacific to flow north of Australia, continuing west below Africa, then turning northwest until reaching eastern Canada, then angling east to southern Europe, then finally turning south just below Greenland and flowing down the Americas' eastern coast, and resuming its flow eastward to complete the circle" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Circulacion_termohalina.jpg/220px-Circulacion_termohalina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Circulacion_termohalina.jpg/330px-Circulacion_termohalina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Circulacion_termohalina.jpg/440px-Circulacion_termohalina.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Circulacion_termohalina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Path of the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a>. Purple paths represent deep-water currents, while blue paths represent surface currents.</div></div></div>
<p>Surface water temperatures, which vary with latitude, current systems, and season and reflect the latitudinal distribution of solar energy, range from below −2 °C (28 °F) to over 30 °C (86 °F). Maximum temperatures occur north of the equator, and minimum values are found in the polar regions. In the middle latitudes, the area of maximum temperature variations, values may vary by 7–8 °C (13–14 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>From October to June the surface is usually covered with sea ice in the <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark_Strait" title="Denmark Strait">Denmark Strait</a>, and Baltic Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a> circulates North Atlantic water in a clockwise direction, whereas South Atlantic water circulates counter-clockwise. The south <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> in the Atlantic Ocean are semi-diurnal; that is, two high tides occur every 24 lunar hours. In latitudes above <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_north" title="40th parallel north">40° North</a> some east–west oscillation, known as the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_oscillation" title="North Atlantic oscillation">North Atlantic oscillation</a>, occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salinity">Salinity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Salinity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On average, the Atlantic is the saltiest major ocean; surface water <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> in the open ocean ranges from 33 to 37 parts per thousand (3.3–3.7%) by mass and varies with latitude and season. Evaporation, precipitation, river inflow and <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a> melting influence surface salinity values. Although the lowest salinity values are just north of the equator (because of heavy tropical rainfall), in general, the lowest values are in the high latitudes and along coasts where large rivers enter. Maximum salinity values occur at about <a href="/wiki/25th_parallel_north" title="25th parallel north">25° north</a> and <a href="/wiki/25th_parallel_south" title="25th parallel south">south</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> regions with low rainfall and high evaporation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The high surface salinity in the Atlantic, on which the Atlantic <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> is dependent, is maintained by two processes: the <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Current#Agulhas_leakage_and_rings" title="Agulhas Current">Agulhas Leakage/Rings</a>, which brings salty Indian Ocean waters into the South Atlantic, and the "Atmospheric Bridge", which evaporates subtropical Atlantic waters and exports it to the Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_masses">Water masses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Water masses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>The Atlantic Ocean consists of four major, upper <a href="/wiki/Water_mass" title="Water mass">water masses</a> with distinct temperature and salinity. The Atlantic Subarctic Upper Water in the northernmost North Atlantic is the source for Subarctic Intermediate Water and North Atlantic Intermediate Water. North Atlantic Central Water can be divided into the Eastern and Western North Atlantic central Water since the western part is strongly affected by the Gulf Stream and therefore the upper layer is closer to underlying fresher subpolar intermediate water. The eastern water is saltier because of its proximity to Mediterranean Water. North Atlantic Central Water flows into South Atlantic Central Water at <a href="/wiki/15th_parallel_north" title="15th parallel north">15°N</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emery-Atlantic_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emery-Atlantic-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are five intermediate waters: four low-salinity waters formed at subpolar latitudes and one high-salinity formed through evaporation. Arctic Intermediate Water, flows from north to become the source for North Atlantic Deep Water south of the Greenland-Scotland sill. These two intermediate waters have different salinity in the western and eastern basins. The wide range of salinities in the North Atlantic is caused by the asymmetry of the northern subtropical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyre" title="Gyre">gyre</a> and the large number of contributions from a wide range of sources: Labrador Sea, Norwegian-Greenland Sea, Mediterranean, and South Atlantic Intermediate Water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emery-Atlantic_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emery-Atlantic-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Deep_Water" title="North Atlantic Deep Water">North Atlantic Deep Water</a> (NADW) is a complex of four water masses, two that form by deep convection in the open ocean — Classical and Upper Labrador Sea Water — and two that form from the inflow of dense water across the Greenland-Iceland-Scotland sill — Denmark Strait and Iceland-Scotland Overflow Water. Along its path across Earth the composition of the NADW is affected by other water masses, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Bottom_Water" title="Antarctic Bottom Water">Antarctic Bottom Water</a> and Mediterranean Overflow Water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
The NADW is fed by a flow of warm shallow water into the northern North Atlantic which is responsible for the anomalous warm climate in Europe. Changes in the formation of NADW have been linked to global climate changes in the past. Since man-made substances were introduced into the environment, the path of the NADW can be traced throughout its course by measuring tritium and radiocarbon from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_test" title="Nuclear weapon test">nuclear weapon tests</a> in the 1960s and <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">CFCs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gyres">Gyres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Gyres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="noresize thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:302px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:194px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">
<div style=";left:0px; top:0px; width:300px; position:absolute;"> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_gyres.png" title="World map of the five major ocean gyres"><img alt="Map showing 5 circles. The first is between western Australia and eastern Africa. The second is between eastern Australia and western South America. The third is between Japan and western North America. Of the two in the Atlantic, one is in hemisphere." data-file-height="1225" data-file-width="1875" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oceanic_gyres.png/300px-Oceanic_gyres.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oceanic_gyres.png/450px-Oceanic_gyres.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oceanic_gyres.png/600px-Oceanic_gyres.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div style="text-align:left; background-color:transparent; line-height:110%;">
<div id="annotation_200x_35" style="position:absolute; left:200px; top: 35px; font-weight:bold; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:black;">North Atlantic    <br/>gyre</span></div>
<div id="annotation_202x_37" style="position:absolute; left:202px; top: 37px; font-weight:bold; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:black;">North Atlantic    <br/>gyre</span></div>
<div id="annotation_201x_36" style="position:absolute; left:201px; top: 36px; font-weight:bold; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:white;">North Atlantic    <br/>gyre</span></div>
<div id="annotation__75x140" style="position:absolute; left: 75px; top:140px; font-weight:bold; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:white;">Indian<br/>Ocean <br/>gyre</span></div>
<div id="annotation_125x_58" style="position:absolute; left:125px; top: 58px; font-weight:bold; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:white;">North<br/>Pacific<br/>gyre</span></div>
<div id="annotation_165x115" style="position:absolute; left:165px; top:115px; font-weight:bold; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:white;">South<br/>Pacific<br/>gyre</span></div>
<div id="annotation_220x160" style="position:absolute; left:220px; top:160px; font-weight:bold; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:white;">South Atlantic<br/>        gyre</span></div>
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_gyres.png"><img alt="Map showing 5 circles. The first is between western Australia and eastern Africa. The second is between eastern Australia and western South America. The third is between Japan and western North America. Of the two in the Atlantic, one is in hemisphere." data-file-height="1225" data-file-width="1875" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oceanic_gyres.png/300px-Oceanic_gyres.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oceanic_gyres.png/450px-Oceanic_gyres.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oceanic_gyres.png/600px-Oceanic_gyres.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_gyres.png" title="File:Oceanic gyres.png"> </a></div>World map of the five major ocean gyres</div>
</div></div>
<p>The clockwise warm-water <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Gyre" title="North Atlantic Gyre">North Atlantic Gyre</a> occupies the northern Atlantic, and the counter-clockwise warm-water <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_Gyre" title="South Atlantic Gyre">South Atlantic Gyre</a> appears in the southern Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the North Atlantic, surface circulation is dominated by three inter-connected currents: the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> which flows north-east from the North American coast at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Hatteras" title="Cape Hatteras">Cape Hatteras</a>; the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a>, a branch of the Gulf Stream which flows northward from the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Banks_of_Newfoundland" title="Grand Banks of Newfoundland">Grand Banks</a>; and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Subpolar_Front&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Subpolar Front (page does not exist)">Subpolar Front</a>, an extension of the North Atlantic Current, a wide, vaguely defined region separating the subtropical gyre from the subpolar gyre. This system of currents transport warm water into the North Atlantic, without which temperatures in the North Atlantic and Europe would plunge dramatically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="465" data-file-width="548" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif/220px-North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif/330px-North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif/440px-North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the subpolar gyre of the North Atlantic warm subtropical waters are transformed into colder subpolar and polar waters. In the Labrador Sea this water flows back to the subtropical gyre.</div></div></div>
<p>North of the North Atlantic Gyre, the cyclonic <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=North_Atlantic_Subpolar_Gyre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="North Atlantic Subpolar Gyre (page does not exist)">North Atlantic Subpolar Gyre</a> plays a key role in climate variability. It is governed by ocean currents from marginal seas and regional topography, rather than being steered by wind, both in the deep ocean and at sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
The subpolar gyre forms an important part of the global <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a>. Its eastern portion includes <a href="/wiki/Eddy_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Eddy (fluid dynamics)">eddying</a> branches of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a> which transport warm, saline waters from the subtropics to the north-eastern Atlantic. There this water is cooled during winter and forms return currents that merge along the eastern continental slope of Greenland where they form an intense (40–50 <a href="/wiki/Sverdrup" title="Sverdrup">Sv</a>) current which flows around the continental margins of the <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a>. A third of this water becomes part of the deep portion of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Deep_Water" title="North Atlantic Deep Water">North Atlantic Deep Water</a> (NADW). The NADW, in its turn, feeds the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meridional_overturning_circulation" title="Meridional overturning circulation">meridional overturning circulation</a> (MOC), the northward heat transport of which is threatened by anthropogenic climate change. Large variations in the subpolar gyre on a decade-century scale, associated with the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_oscillation" title="North Atlantic oscillation">North Atlantic oscillation</a>, are especially pronounced in <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea_Water" title="Labrador Sea Water">Labrador Sea Water</a>, the upper layers of the MOC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The South Atlantic is dominated by the anti-cyclonic southern subtropical gyre. The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=South_Atlantic_Central_Water&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="South Atlantic Central Water (page does not exist)">South Atlantic Central Water</a> originates in this gyre, while <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Intermediate_Water" title="Antarctic Intermediate Water">Antarctic Intermediate Water</a> originates in the upper layers of the circumpolar region, near the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a> and the Falkland Islands. Both these currents receive some contribution from the Indian Ocean. On the African east coast, the small cyclonic <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Angola_Gyre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Angola Gyre (page does not exist)">Angola Gyre</a> lies embedded in the large subtropical gyre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
The southern subtropical gyre is partly masked by a wind-induced <a href="/wiki/Ekman_layer" title="Ekman layer">Ekman layer</a>. The residence time of the gyre is 4.4–8.5 years. <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Deep_Water" title="North Atlantic Deep Water">North Atlantic Deep Water</a> flows southward below the <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a> of the subtropical gyre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sargasso_Sea">Sargasso Sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sargasso Sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
