//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HanaOZ.jpg/400px-HanaOZ.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Sargassum fish (<i>Histrio histrio</i>)</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The Sargasso Sea in the western North Atlantic can be defined as the area where two species of <i><a href="/wiki/Sargassum" title="Sargassum">Sargassum</a></i> (<i>S. fluitans</i> and <i>natans</i>) float, an area 4,000 km (2,500 mi) wide and encircled by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Drift" title="North Atlantic Drift">North Atlantic Drift</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_Equatorial_Current" title="North Equatorial Current">North Equatorial Current</a>. This population of seaweed probably originated from Tertiary ancestors on the European shores of the former <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a> and has, if so, maintained itself by <a href="/wiki/Vegetative_reproduction" title="Vegetative reproduction">vegetative growth</a>, floating in the ocean for millions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lün-p223_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lün-p223-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other species endemic to the Sargasso Sea include the <a href="/wiki/Sargassum_fish" title="Sargassum fish">sargassum fish</a>, a predator with algae-like appendages which hovers motionless among the <i>Sargassum</i>. Fossils of similar fishes have been found in fossil bays of the former Tethys Ocean, in what is now the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian</a> region, that were similar to the Sargasso Sea. It is possible that the population in the Sargasso Sea migrated to the Atlantic as the Tethys closed at the end of the Miocene around 17 Ma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lün-p223_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lün-p223-44">[44]</a></sup> The origin of the Sargasso fauna and flora remained enigmatic for centuries. The fossils found in the Carpathians in the mid-20th century often called the "quasi-Sargasso assemblage", finally showed that this assemblage originated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpathian_Basin" title="Carpathian Basin">Carpathian Basin</a> from where it migrated over <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> to the Central Atlantic where it evolved into modern species of the Sargasso Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The location of the spawning ground for European eels <a href="/wiki/Eel_life_history#Search_for_the_spawning_grounds" title="Eel life history">remained unknown for decades</a>. In the early 19th century it was discovered that the southern Sargasso Sea is the spawning ground for both the <a href="/wiki/European_eel" title="European eel">European</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_eel" title="American eel">American eel</a> and that the former migrate more than 5,000 km (3,100 mi) and the latter 2,000 km (1,200 mi). Ocean currents such as the Gulf Stream transport eel larvae from the Sargasso Sea to foraging areas in North America, Europe, and Northern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Recent but disputed research suggests that eels possibly use <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> to navigate through the ocean both as larvae and as adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png"><img alt="Map of Caribbean showing seven approximately parallel westward-pointing arrows that extend from east of the Virgin Islands to Cuba. The southern arrows bend northward just east of the Dominican Republic before straightening out again." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png/220px-Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png/330px-Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Waves in the <a href="/wiki/Trade_winds" title="Trade winds">trade winds</a> in the Atlantic Ocean—areas of converging winds that move along the same track as the prevailing wind—create instabilities in the atmosphere that may lead to the formation of hurricanes.</div></div></div>
<p>Climate is influenced by the temperatures of the surface waters and water currents as well as winds. Because of the ocean's great capacity to store and release heat, maritime climates are more moderate and have less extreme seasonal variations than inland climates. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">Precipitation</a> can be approximated from coastal weather data and air temperature from water temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oceans are the major source of the atmospheric moisture that is obtained through evaporation. Climatic zones vary with latitude; the warmest zones stretch across the Atlantic north of the equator. The coldest zones are in high latitudes, with the coldest regions corresponding to the areas covered by sea ice. Ocean currents influence the climate by transporting warm and cold waters to other regions. The winds that are cooled or warmed when blowing over these currents influence adjacent land areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> and its northern extension towards Europe, the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Drift</a> is thought to have at least some influence on climate. For example, the Gulf Stream helps moderate winter temperatures along the coastline of southeastern North America, keeping it warmer in winter along the coast than inland areas. The Gulf Stream also keeps extreme temperatures from occurring on the Florida Peninsula. In the higher latitudes, the North Atlantic Drift, warms the atmosphere over the oceans, keeping the British Isles and north-western Europe mild and cloudy, and not severely cold in winter, like other locations at the same high latitude. The cold water currents contribute to heavy fog off the coast of eastern Canada (the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Banks_of_Newfoundland" title="Grand Banks of Newfoundland">Grand Banks of Newfoundland</a> area) and Africa's north-western coast. In general, winds transport moisture and air over land areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_hazards">Natural hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Natural hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iceberg_A22A,_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1553" data-file-width="2345" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg/220px-Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg/330px-Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg/440px-Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iceberg_A22A,_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iceberg A22A in the South Atlantic Ocean</div></div></div>
<p>Every winter, the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Low" title="Icelandic Low">Icelandic Low</a> produces frequent storms. <a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">Icebergs</a> are common from early February to the end of July across the shipping lanes near the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Banks_of_Newfoundland" title="Grand Banks of Newfoundland">Grand Banks of Newfoundland</a>. The ice season is longer in the polar regions, but there is little shipping in those areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane" title="Atlantic hurricane">Hurricanes</a> are a hazard in the western parts of the North Atlantic during the summer and autumn. Due to a consistently strong <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> and a weak <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_tropical_cyclone" title="South Atlantic tropical cyclone">South Atlantic tropical cyclones</a> are rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology_and_plate_tectonics">Geology and plate tectonics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geology and plate tectonics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Atlantic Ocean is underlain mostly by dense <a href="/wiki/Mafic" title="Mafic">mafic</a> oceanic crust made up of <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gabbro" title="Gabbro">gabbro</a> and overlain by fine clay, silt and siliceous ooze on the abyssal plain. The continental margins and continental shelf mark lower density, but greater thickness <a href="/wiki/Felsic" title="Felsic">felsic</a> continental rock that is often much older than that of the seafloor. The oldest oceanic crust in the Atlantic is up to 145 million years and situated off the west coast of Africa and east coast of North America, or on either side of the South Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many places, the continental shelf and continental slope are covered in thick sedimentary layers. For instance, on the North American side of the ocean, large carbonate deposits formed in warm shallow waters such as Florida and the Bahamas, while coarse river outwash sands and silt are common in shallow shelf areas like the <a href="/wiki/Georges_Bank" title="Georges Bank">Georges Bank</a>. Coarse sand, boulders, and rocks were transported into some areas, such as off the coast of Nova Scotia or the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Maine" title="Gulf of Maine">Gulf of Maine</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> ice ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Atlantic">Central Atlantic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Central Atlantic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blakey_200Ma_-_COL.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="189" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Blakey_200Ma_-_COL.jpg/180px-Blakey_200Ma_-_COL.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Blakey_200Ma_-_COL.jpg 1.5x" width="180"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="216" data-file-width="273" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg/180px-Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg/270px-Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Opening of the Central Atlantic 200-170 Ma</div></div></div></div>
<p>The break-up of Pangaea began in the Central Atlantic, between North America and Northwest Africa, where rift basins opened during the Late Triassic and Early Jurassic. This period also saw the first stages of the uplift of the Atlas Mountains. The exact timing is controversial with estimates ranging from 200 to 170 Ma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The opening of the Atlantic Ocean coincided with the initial break-up of the supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>, both of which were initiated by the eruption of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Atlantic_Magmatic_Province" title="Central Atlantic Magmatic Province">Central Atlantic Magmatic Province</a> (CAMP), one of the most extensive and voluminous <a href="/wiki/Large_igneous_province" title="Large igneous province">large igneous provinces</a> in Earth's history associated with the <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">Triassic–Jurassic extinction event</a>, one of Earth's major <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">extinction events</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
Theoliitic <a href="/wiki/Dike_(geology)" title="Dike (geology)">dikes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_sill" title="Aquatic sill">sills</a>, and lava flows from the CAMP eruption at 200 Ma have been found in West Africa, eastern North America, and northern South America. The extent of the volcanism has been estimated to 4.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>6</sup> km<sup>2</sup> (1.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>6</sup> sq mi) of which 2.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>6</sup> km<sup>2</sup> (9.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>5</sup> sq mi) covered what is now northern and central Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_American_Isthmus" title="Central American Isthmus">Central American Isthmus</a> closed the <a href="/wiki/Central_American_Seaway" title="Central American Seaway">Central American Seaway</a> at the end of the Pliocene 2.8 Ma ago. The formation of the isthmus resulted in the migration and extinction of many land-living animals, known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Interchange" title="Great American Interchange">Great American Interchange</a>, but the closure of the seaway resulted in a "Great American Schism" as it affected ocean currents, salinity, and temperatures in both the Atlantic and Pacific. Marine organisms on both sides of the isthmus became isolated and either diverged or went extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Atlantic"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023754711">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="North_Atlantic"></span><span id="NORTH_ATLANTIC"></span><span class="vanchor-text">North Atlantic</span></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: North Atlantic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Opening_of_the_North_Atlantic_Ocean" title="Opening of the North Atlantic Ocean">Opening of the North Atlantic Ocean</a></div>
<p>Geologically, the Northern Atlantic is the area delimited to the south by two conjugate margins, Newfoundland and Iberia, and to the north by the Arctic <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Basin" title="Eurasian Basin">Eurasian Basin</a>. The opening of the Northern Atlantic closely followed the margins of its predecessor, the <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_Ocean" title="Iapetus Ocean">Iapetus Ocean</a>, and spread from the Central Atlantic in six stages: <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberia</a>–<a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porcupine_Bank" title="Porcupine Bank">Porcupine</a>–North America, Eurasia–Greenland, Eurasia–North America. Active and inactive spreading systems in this area are marked by the interaction with the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_hotspot" title="Iceland hotspot">Iceland hotspot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Seafloor spreading led to the extension of the crust and formations of troughs and sedimentary basins. The Rockall Trough opened between 105 and 84 million years ago although along the rift failed along with one leading into the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Biscay" title="Bay of Biscay">Bay of Biscay</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFittonLarsen199915_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFittonLarsen199915-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Spreading began opening the <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a> around 61 million years ago, continuing until 36 million years ago. Geologists distinguish two magmatic phases. One from 62 to 58 million years ago predates the separation of Greenland from northern Europe while the second from 56 to 52 million years ago happened as the separation occurred.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iceland_Plateau" title="Iceland Plateau">Iceland</a> began to form 62 million years ago due to a particularly concentrated mantle plume. Large quantities of <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> erupted at this time period are found on Baffin Island, Greenland, the Faroe Islands, and Scotland, with ash falls in Western Europe acting as a stratigraphic marker. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFittonLarsen199910_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFittonLarsen199910-58">[58]</a></sup> The opening of the North Atlantic caused significant uplift of continental crust along the coast. For instance, in spite of 7 km thick basalt, Gunnbjorn Field in East Greenland is the highest point on the island, elevated enough that it exposes older Mesozoic sedimentary rocks at its base, similar to old lava fields above sedimentary rocks in the uplifted Hebrides of western Scotland. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFittonLarsen199923-24_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFittonLarsen199923-24-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The North Atlantic Ocean contains about 810 <a href="/wiki/Seamount" title="Seamount">seamounts</a>, most of them situated along the Mid-Atlantic Ridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/OSPAR_Convention" title="OSPAR Convention">OSPAR</a> database (Convention for the Protection of the Marine Environment of the North-East Atlantic) mentions 104 seamounts: 74 within the national <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">Exclusive economic zone</a>. Of these seamounts, 46 are located close to the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Atlantic">South Atlantic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: South Atlantic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gondwana115Ma_hg.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Gondwana115Ma_hg.png/180px-Gondwana115Ma_hg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Gondwana115Ma_hg.png/270px-Gondwana115Ma_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Gondwana115Ma_hg.png/360px-Gondwana115Ma_hg.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gondwana065Ma_hg.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Gondwana065Ma_hg.png/180px-Gondwana065Ma_hg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Gondwana065Ma_hg.png/270px-Gondwana065Ma_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Gondwana065Ma_hg.png/360px-Gondwana065Ma_hg.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gondwana038ma_hg.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Gondwana038ma_hg.png/180px-Gondwana038ma_hg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Gondwana038ma_hg.png/270px-Gondwana038ma_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Gondwana038ma_hg.png/360px-Gondwana038ma_hg.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The opening of the South Atlantic</div></div></div></div>
<p>West Gondwana (South America and Africa) broke up in the Early Cretaceous to form the South Atlantic. The apparent fit between the coastlines of the two continents was noted on the first maps that included the South Atlantic and it was also the subject of the first computer-assisted plate tectonic reconstructions in 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eagles-intro_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eagles-intro-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> This magnificent fit, however, has since then proven problematic and later reconstructions have introduced various deformation zones along the shorelines to accommodate the northward-propagating break-up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eagles-intro_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eagles-intro-61">[61]</a></sup> Intra-continental rifts and deformations have also been introduced to subdivide both continental plates into sub-plates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seton-2012-p217_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seton-2012-p217-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Geologically the South Atlantic can be divided into four segments: Equatorial segment, from 10°N to the Romanche Fracture Zone (RFZ); Central segment, from RFZ to Florianopolis Fracture Zone (FFZ, north of Walvis Ridge and Rio Grande Rise); Southern segment, from FFZ to the Agulhas-Falkland Fracture Zone (AFFZ); and Falkland segment, south of AFFZ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torsvik2009-p1316_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Torsvik2009-p1316-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the southern segment the Early Cretaceous (133–130 Ma) intensive <a href="/wiki/Magmatism" title="Magmatism">magmatism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_and_Etendeka_traps" title="Paraná and Etendeka traps">Paraná–Etendeka Large Igneous Province</a> produced by the <a href="/wiki/Tristan_hotspot" title="Tristan hotspot">Tristan hotspot</a> resulted in an estimated volume of 1.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>6</sup> to 2.0<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>6</sup> km<sup>3</sup> (3.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>5</sup> to 4.8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>5</sup> cu mi). It covered an area of 1.2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>6</sup> to 1.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>6</sup> km<sup>2</sup> (4.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>5</sup> to 6.2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>5</sup> sq mi) in Brazil, Paraguay, and Uruguay and 0.8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>5</sup> km<sup>2</sup> (3.1<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>4</sup> sq mi) in Africa. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyke_swarm" title="Dyke swarm">Dyke swarms</a> in Brazil, Angola, eastern Paraguay, and Namibia, however, suggest the LIP originally covered a much larger area and also indicate failed rifts in all these areas. Associated offshore basaltic flows reach as far south as the Falkland Islands and South Africa. Traces of magmatism in both offshore and onshore basins in the central and southern segments have been dated to 147–49 Ma with two peaks between 143 and 121 Ma and 90–60 Ma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torsvik2009-p1316_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Torsvik2009-p1316-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Falkland segment rifting began with dextral movements between the Patagonia and Colorado sub-plates between the Early Jurassic (190 Ma) and the Early Cretaceous (126.7 Ma). Around 150 Ma sea-floor spreading propagated northward into the southern segment. No later than 130 Ma rifting had reached the Walvis Ridge–Rio Grande Rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seton-2012-p217_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seton-2012-p217-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the central segment rifting started to break Africa in two by opening the <a href="/wiki/Benue_Trough" title="Benue Trough">Benue Trough</a> around 118 Ma. Rifting in the central segment, however, coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal#Superchrons" title="Geomagnetic reversal">Cretaceous Normal Superchron</a> (also known as the Cretaceous quiet period), a 40 Ma period without magnetic reversals, which makes it difficult to date sea-floor spreading in this segment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seton-2012-p217_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Seton-2012-p217-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The equatorial segment is the last phase of the break-up, but, because it is located on the Equator, magnetic anomalies cannot be used for dating. Various estimates date the propagation of sea-floor spreading in this segment to the period 120–96 Ma. This final stage, nevertheless, coincided with or resulted in the end of continental extension in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seton-2012-p217_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-Seton-2012-p217-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 50 Ma the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a> resulted from a change in the motions and separation rate of the South American and Antarctic plates. First small ocean basins opened and a shallow gateway appeared during the Middle Eocene. 34–30 Ma a deeper seaway developed, followed by an <a href="/wiki/Eocene%E2%80%93Oligocene_extinction_event" title="Eocene–Oligocene extinction event">Eocene–Oligocene climatic deterioration</a> and the growth of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Closure_of_the_Atlantic">Closure of the Atlantic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Closure of the Atlantic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pangaea_Proxima" title="Pangaea Proxima">Pangaea Proxima</a></div>
<p>An embryonic subduction margin is potentially developing west of Gibraltar. The <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar_Arc" title="Gibraltar Arc">Gibraltar Arc</a> in the western Mediterranean is migrating westward into the Central Atlantic where it joins the converging African and Eurasian plates. Together these three tectonic forces are slowly developing into a new subduction system in the eastern Atlantic Basin. Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Scotia_Arc" title="Scotia Arc">Scotia Arc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Plate" title="Caribbean Plate">Caribbean Plate</a> in the western Atlantic Basin are eastward-propagating subduction systems that might, together with the Gibraltar system, represent the beginning of the closure of the Atlantic Ocean and the final stage of the Atlantic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilson_cycle" title="Wilson cycle">Wilson cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main category: <a href="/wiki/Category:History_of_the_Atlantic_Ocean" title="Category:History of the Atlantic Ocean">History of the Atlantic Ocean</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_origin">Human origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Human origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Human_evolution" title="Human evolution">Humans evolved</a> in Africa; first by diverging from other apes around 7 mya; then developing stone tools around 2.6 mya; to finally evolve as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_human" title="Anatomically modern human">modern humans</a> around 200 kya. The earliest evidence for the complex behavior associated with this <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">behavioral modernity</a> has been found in the Greater <a href="/wiki/Cape_Floristic_Region" title="Cape Floristic Region">Cape Floristic Region</a> (GCFR) along the coast of South Africa. During the latest glacial stages, the now-submerged plains of the <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Bank" title="Agulhas Bank">Agulhas Bank</a> were exposed above sea level, extending the South African coastline farther south by hundreds of kilometers. A small population of modern humans — probably fewer than a thousand reproducing individuals — survived glacial maxima by exploring the high diversity offered by these Palaeo-Agulhas plains. The GCFR is delimited to the north by the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Fold_Belt" title="Cape Fold Belt">Cape Fold Belt</a> and the limited space south of it resulted in the development of social networks out of which complex Stone Age technologies emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Human history thus begins on the coasts of South Africa where the Atlantic <a href="/wiki/Benguela_Current" title="Benguela Current">Benguela Upwelling</a> and Indian Ocean <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Current" title="Agulhas Current">Agulhas Current</a> meet to produce an intertidal zone on which shellfish, fur seal, fish and sea birds provided the necessary protein sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
The African origin of this modern behaviour is evidenced by 70,000 years-old engravings from <a href="/wiki/Blombos_Cave" title="Blombos Cave">Blombos Cave</a>, South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_World">Old World</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Old World">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">Mitochondrial DNA</a> (mtDNA) studies indicate that 80–60,000 years ago a major demographic expansion within Africa, derived from a single, small population, coincided with the emergence of behavioral complexity and the rapid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_isotope_stage" title="Marine isotope stage">MIS</a> 5–4 environmental changes. This group of people not only expanded over the whole of Africa, but also started to disperse <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">out of Africa</a> into Asia, Europe, and Australasia around 65,000 years ago and quickly replaced the archaic humans in these regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> (LGM) 20,000 years ago humans had to abandon their initial settlements along the European North Atlantic coast and retreat to the Mediterranean. Following rapid climate changes at the end of the LGM this region was repopulated by <a href="/wiki/Magdalenian" title="Magdalenian">Magdalenian</a> culture. Other hunter-gatherers followed in waves interrupted by large-scale hazards such as the <a href="/wiki/Laacher_See" title="Laacher See">Laacher See</a> volcanic eruption, the inundation of <a href="/wiki/Doggerland" title="Doggerland">Doggerland</a> (now the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>), and the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The European coasts of the North Atlantic were permanently populated about 9–8.5 thousand years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>This human dispersal left abundant traces along the coasts of the Atlantic Ocean. 50 kya-old, deeply stratified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shell_midden" title="Shell midden">shell middens</a> found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ysterfontein" title="Ysterfontein">Ysterfontein</a> on the western coast of South Africa are associated with the Middle Stone Age (MSA). The MSA population was small and dispersed and the rate of their reproduction and exploitation was less intense than those of later generations. While their middens resemble 12–11 kya-old Late Stone Age (LSA) middens found on every inhabited continent, the 50–45 kya-old <a href="/wiki/Enkapune_Ya_Muto" title="Enkapune Ya Muto">Enkapune Ya Muto</a> in Kenya probably represents the oldest traces of the first modern humans to disperse out of Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meilgaard-boplads.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="353" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Meilgaard-boplads.jpg/220px-Meilgaard-boplads.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Meilgaard-boplads.jpg/330px-Meilgaard-boplads.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Meilgaard-boplads.jpg/440px-Meilgaard-boplads.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meilgaard-boplads.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Excavation of the <a href="/wiki/Erteb%C3%B8lle_culture" title="Ertebølle culture">Ertebølle</a> middens in 1880</div></div></div>
<p>The same development can be seen in Europe. In <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=La_Riera_Cave&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="La Riera Cave (page does not exist)">La Riera Cave</a> (23–13 kya) in Asturias, Spain, only some 26,600 molluscs were deposited over 10 kya. In contrast, 8–7 kya-old shell middens in Portugal, Denmark, and Brazil generated thousands of tons of debris and artefacts. The <a href="/wiki/Erteb%C3%B8lle_culture" title="Ertebølle culture">Ertebølle</a> middens in Denmark, for example, accumulated 2,000 m<sup>3</sup> (71,000 cu ft) of shell deposits representing some 50 million molluscs over only a thousand years. This intensification in the exploitation of marine resources has been described as accompanied by new technologies — such as boats, harpoons, and fish-hooks — because many caves found in the Mediterranean and on the European Atlantic coast have increased quantities of marine shells in their upper levels and reduced quantities in their lower. The earliest exploitation, however, took place on the now submerged shelves, and most settlements now excavated were then located several kilometers from these shelves. The reduced quantities of shells in the lower levels can represent the few shells that were exported inland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_World">New World</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: New World">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Brendan#Tale_of_reaching_North_America" title="Brendan">Brendan of Clonfert</a></div>
<p>During the LGM the <a href="/wiki/Laurentide_Ice_Sheet" title="Laurentide Ice Sheet">Laurentide Ice Sheet</a> covered most of northern North America while <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringia</a> connected Siberia to Alaska. In 1973, late American geoscientist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Schultz_Martin" title="Paul Schultz Martin">Paul S. Martin</a> proposed a "blitzkrieg" colonization of the Americas by which <a href="/wiki/Clovis_culture" title="Clovis culture">Clovis hunters</a> migrated into North America around 13,000 years ago in a single wave through an ice-free corridor in the ice sheet and "spread southward explosively, briefly attaining a density sufficiently large to overkill much of their prey."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Others later proposed a "three-wave" migration over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bering_Land_Bridge" title="Bering Land Bridge">Bering Land Bridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> These hypotheses remained the long-held view regarding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_migration_and_settlement_of_the_Americas_from_Asia" title="Prehistoric migration and settlement of the Americas from Asia">settlement of the Americas</a>, a view challenged by more recent archaeological discoveries: the oldest archaeological sites in the Americas have been found in South America; sites in north-east Siberia report virtually no human presence there during the LGM; and most Clovis artefacts have been found in eastern North America along the Atlantic coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Furthermore, colonisation models based on mtDNA, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YDNA" title="YDNA">yDNA</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AtDNA" title="AtDNA">atDNA</a> data respectively support neither the "blitzkrieg" nor the "three-wave" hypotheses but they also deliver mutually ambiguous results. Contradictory data from archaeology and genetics will most likely deliver future hypotheses that will, eventually, confirm each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> A proposed route across the Pacific to South America could explain early South American finds and another hypothesis proposes a northern path, through the Canadian Arctic and down the North American Atlantic coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
Early settlements across the Atlantic have been suggested by alternative theories, ranging from purely hypothetical to mostly disputed, including the <a href="/wiki/Solutrean_hypothesis" title="Solutrean hypothesis">Solutrean hypothesis</a> and some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_trans-oceanic_contact_theories" title="Pre-Columbian trans-oceanic contact theories">Pre-Columbian trans-oceanic contact theories</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Norse-world.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="666" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Norse-world.png/220px-Norse-world.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Norse-world.png/330px-Norse-world.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Norse-world.png/440px-Norse-world.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Norse-world.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Based on the medieval <i><a href="/wiki/Sagas_of_Icelanders" title="Sagas of Icelanders">Íslendingasögur</a></i> sagas, including the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenland_saga" title="Greenland saga">Grœnlendinga saga</a></i>, this interpretative map of the "Norse World" shows that Norse knowledge of the Americas and the Atlantic remained limited.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_North_America" title="Norse colonization of North America">Norse settlement</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> began during the 9th and 10th centuries. A settlement on <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> was established before 1000 CE, but contact with it was lost in 1409 and it was finally abandoned during the early <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>. This setback was caused by a range of factors: an unsustainable economy resulted in erosion and denudation, while conflicts with the local <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> resulted in the failure to adapt their Arctic technologies; a colder climate resulted in starvation, and the colony got economically marginalized as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Plague" title="Great Plague">Great Plague</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">Barbary pirates</a> harvested its victims on Iceland in the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
Iceland was initially settled 865–930 CE following a warm period when winter temperatures hovered around 2 °C (36 °F) which made farming favorable at high latitudes. This did not last, however, and temperatures quickly dropped; at 1080 CE summer temperatures had reached a maximum of 5 °C (41 °F). The <i><a href="/wiki/Landn%C3%A1mab%C3%B3k" title="Landnámabók">Landnámabók</a></i> (<i>Book of Settlement</i>) records disastrous famines during the first century of settlement — "men ate <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raven" title="Raven">ravens</a>" and "the old and helpless were killed and thrown over cliffs" — and by the early 1200s hay had to be abandoned for short-season crops such as <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_World">Atlantic World</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Atlantic World">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_world" title="Atlantic world">Atlantic world</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_history" title="Atlantic history">Atlantic history</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Portuguese_Carreira_da_India.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="1047" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Map_of_Portuguese_Carreira_da_India.gif/220px-Map_of_Portuguese_Carreira_da_India.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Map_of_Portuguese_Carreira_da_India.gif/330px-Map_of_Portuguese_Carreira_da_India.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Map_of_Portuguese_Carreira_da_India.gif/440px-Map_of_Portuguese_Carreira_da_India.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Portuguese_Carreira_da_India.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Atlantic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyre" title="Gyre">Gyres</a> influenced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">Portuguese discoveries</a> and trading port routes, here shown in the India Run ("<i>Carreira da Índia</i>"), which would be developed in subsequent years.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">reached the Americas</a> in 1492 under Spanish flag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamb-184_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamb-184-82">[82]</a></sup> Six years later <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> reached India under the Portuguese flag, by navigating south around the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>, thus proving that the Atlantic and Indian Oceans are connected. In 1500, in his voyage to India following Vasco da Gama, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedro_Alvares_Cabral" title="Pedro Alvares Cabral">Pedro Alvares Cabral</a> reached Brazil, taken by the currents of the <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_Gyre" title="South Atlantic Gyre">South Atlantic Gyre</a>. Following these explorations, Spain and Portugal quickly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Colonization of the Americas">conquered and colonized</a> large territories in the New World and forced the Amerindian population into slavery in order to exploit the vast quantities of silver and gold they found. Spain and Portugal monopolized this trade in order to keep other European nations out, but conflicting interests nevertheless led to a series of Spanish-Portuguese wars. A peace treaty mediated by the Pope divided the conquered territories into Spanish and Portuguese sectors while keeping other colonial powers away. England, France, and the Dutch Republic enviously watched the Spanish and Portuguese wealth grow and allied themselves with <a href="/wiki/Piracy_in_the_Atlantic_World" title="Piracy in the Atlantic World">pirates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Mainwaring" title="Henry Mainwaring">Henry Mainwaring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Exquemelin" title="Alexandre Exquemelin">Alexandre Exquemelin</a>. They could explore the convoys leaving the Americas because prevailing winds and currents made the transport of heavy metals slow and predictable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamb-184_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chamb-184-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_slavetrade_estimates.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="731" data-file-width="1146" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Atlantic_slavetrade_estimates.png/220px-Atlantic_slavetrade_estimates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Atlantic_slavetrade_estimates.png/330px-Atlantic_slavetrade_estimates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Atlantic_slavetrade_estimates.png/440px-Atlantic_slavetrade_estimates.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_slavetrade_estimates.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Embarked and disembarked slaves in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a> 1525–1863 (first and last slave voyages)</div></div></div>
<p>In the colonies of the Americas, depredation, <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> and others diseases, and <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> quickly reduced the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous population of the Americas</a> to the extent that the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a> had to be introduced to replace them — a trade that became the norm and an integral part of the colonization. Between the 15th century and 1888, when <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Brazil" title="Slavery in Brazil">Brazil</a> became the last part of the Americas to end the slave trade, an estimated ten million Africans were exported as slaves, most of them destined for agricultural labour. The slave trade was officially abolished in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_British_Isles" title="Slavery in the British Isles">British Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">United States</a> in 1808, and slavery itself was abolished in the British Empire in 1838 and in the United States in 1865 after the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>From Columbus to the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> Trans-Atlantic trade, including colonialism and slavery, became crucial for Western Europe. For European countries with direct access to the Atlantic (including Britain, France, the Netherlands, Portugal, and Spain) 1500–1800 was a period of sustained growth during which these countries grew richer than those in Eastern Europe and Asia. Colonialism evolved as part of the Trans-Atlantic trade, but this trade also strengthened the position of merchant groups at the expense of monarchs. Growth was more rapid in non-absolutist countries, such as Britain and the Netherlands, and more limited in <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolutist monarchies</a>, such as Portugal, Spain, and France, where profit mostly or exclusively benefited the monarchy and its allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acem-p546_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Acem-p546-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trans-Atlantic trade also resulted in increasing urbanization: in European countries facing the Atlantic, urbanization grew from 8% in 1300, 10.1% in 1500, to 24.5% in 1850; in other European countries from 10% in 1300, 11.4% in 1500, to 17% in 1850. Likewise, GDP doubled in Atlantic countries but rose by only 30% in the rest of Europe. By end of the 17th century, the volume of the Trans-Atlantic trade had surpassed that of the Mediterranean trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acem-p546_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Acem-p546-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Atlantic Ocean became the scene of one of the longest continuous naval military camapaigns throughout <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, from 1939 to 1945.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_notable_crossings_of_the_Atlantic_Ocean" title="List of notable crossings of the Atlantic Ocean">List of notable crossings of the Atlantic Ocean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">Battle of the Atlantic</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Atlantic has contributed significantly to the development and economy of surrounding countries. Besides major transatlantic transportation and communication routes, the Atlantic offers abundant petroleum deposits in the <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a> of the continental shelves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kanstadsamlingen_-_NMF010005-01347.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8696" data-file-width="8710" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kanstadsamlingen_-_NMF010005-01347.jpg/220px-Kanstadsamlingen_-_NMF010005-01347.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kanstadsamlingen_-_NMF010005-01347.jpg/330px-Kanstadsamlingen_-_NMF010005-01347.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kanstadsamlingen_-_NMF010005-01347.jpg/440px-Kanstadsamlingen_-_NMF010005-01347.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kanstadsamlingen_-_NMF010005-01347.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cod_fisheries" title="Cod fisheries">Cod fishery</a> in Norway</div></div></div>
<p>The Atlantic harbors petroleum and gas fields, fish, <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammals</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a> and whales), <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">sand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gravel" title="Gravel">gravel</a> aggregates, <a href="/wiki/Placer_deposit" title="Placer deposit">placer deposits</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polymetallic_nodules" title="Polymetallic nodules">polymetallic nodules</a>, and precious stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
Gold deposits are a mile or two under water on the ocean floor, however, the deposits are also encased in rock that must be mined through. Currently, there is no cost-effective way to mine or extract gold from the ocean to make a profit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Various international treaties attempt to reduce pollution caused by environmental threats such as oil spills, <a href="/wiki/Marine_debris" title="Marine debris">marine debris</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Incineration" title="Incineration">incineration</a> of toxic wastes at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fisheries">Fisheries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Fisheries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">shelves</a> of the Atlantic hosts one of the world's richest <a href="/wiki/Wild_fisheries" title="Wild fisheries">fishing resources</a>. The most productive areas include the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Banks_of_Newfoundland" title="Grand Banks of Newfoundland">Grand Banks of Newfoundland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scotian_Shelf" title="Scotian Shelf">Scotian Shelf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Bank" title="Georges Bank">Georges Bank</a> off <a href="/wiki/Cape_Cod" title="Cape Cod">Cape Cod</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bahama_Banks" title="Bahama Banks">Bahama Banks</a>, the waters around Iceland, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Fundy" title="Bay of Fundy">Bay of Fundy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dogger_Bank" title="Dogger Bank">Dogger Bank</a> of the North Sea, and the Falkland Banks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN-2001_6-12"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-6">[6]</a></sup>
Fisheries have, however, undergone significant changes since the 1950s and global catches can now be divided into three groups of which only two are observed in the Atlantic: fisheries in the Eastern Central and South-West Atlantic oscillate around a globally stable value, the rest of the Atlantic is in overall decline following historical peaks. The third group, "continuously increasing trend since 1950", is only found in the Indian Ocean and Western Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOA-2016-p39_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOA-2016-p39-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>UN <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FAO" title="FAO">FAO</a> partitioned Atlantic in major fishing areas:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Northeast_Atlantic_bathymetry.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="323" data-file-width="413" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Northeast_Atlantic_bathymetry.png/220px-Northeast_Atlantic_bathymetry.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Northeast_Atlantic_bathymetry.png/330px-Northeast_Atlantic_bathymetry.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Northeast_Atlantic_bathymetry.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Northeast_Atlantic_bathymetry.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Banks of the North-East Atlantic</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>North-East Atlantic</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<p>North-East Atlantic is schematically limited to the 42°00' west longitude except around Greenland and at south to 36°00' north latitude; and 68°30' east longitude at the east, according to FAO fisheries definition and include various seas and/or subareas: <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian Sea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spitzbergen" title="Spitzbergen">Spitzbergen</a>,  <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Bear_Island" title="Bear Island">Bear Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Skagerrak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kattegat" title="Kattegat">Kattegat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">Sound</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Belt_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Belt Sea (page does not exist)">Belt Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> , <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Faroes_Grounds&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Faroes Grounds (page does not exist)">Faroes Grounds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rockall" title="Rockall">Rockall</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Northwest_Coast_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Northwest Coast of Scotland (page does not exist)">Northwest Coast of Scotland</a>, North Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a>, West of Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Porcupine_Bank" title="Porcupine Bank">Porcupine Bank</a>, Eastern and Western <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Biscay" title="Bay of Biscay">Bay of Biscay</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Portuguese_Waters&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Portuguese Waters (page does not exist)">Portuguese Waters</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Azores_Grounds&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Azores Grounds (page does not exist)">Azores Grounds</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Northeast_Atlantic_South&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Northeast Atlantic South (page does not exist)">Northeast Atlantic South</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>In the North-East Atlantic total catches decreased between the mid-1970s and the 1990s and reached 8.7 million tons in 2013. <a href="/wiki/Blue_whiting" title="Blue whiting">Blue whiting</a> reached a 2.4 million tons peak in 2004 but was down to 628,000 tons in 2013. Recovery plans for cod, sole, and plaice have reduced mortality in these species. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_cod" title="Arctic cod">Arctic cod</a> reached its lowest levels in the 1960s–1980s but is now recovered. <a href="/wiki/Pollachius_virens" title="Pollachius virens">Arctic saithe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haddock" title="Haddock">haddock</a> are considered fully fished; <a href="/wiki/Sand_eel" title="Sand eel">Sand eel</a> is overfished as was <a href="/wiki/Capelin" title="Capelin">capelin</a> which has now recovered to fully fished. Limited data makes the state of <a href="/wiki/Redfish" title="Redfish">redfishes</a> and deep-water species difficult to assess but most likely they remain vulnerable to overfishing. Stocks of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_shrimp" title="Northern shrimp">northern shrimp</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norwegian_lobster" title="Norwegian lobster">Norwegian lobster</a> are in good condition. In the North-East Atlantic 21% of stocks are considered overfished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOA-2016-p39_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOA-2016-p39-88">[88]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>This zone makes almost three quarters (72.8 %) of <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> fishing catches in 2020. Main fishing EU countries are Denmark, France, the Netherlands and Spain. Most common species include <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mackerel" title="Mackerel">mackerel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sprat" title="Sprat">sprats</a>.</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:StLawrence_Gulf_bathymetry.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="505" data-file-width="820" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/StLawrence_Gulf_bathymetry.jpg/220px-StLawrence_Gulf_bathymetry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/StLawrence_Gulf_bathymetry.jpg/330px-StLawrence_Gulf_bathymetry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/StLawrence_Gulf_bathymetry.jpg/440px-StLawrence_Gulf_bathymetry.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:StLawrence_Gulf_bathymetry.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Banks of the North-West Atlantic</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>North-West Atlantic</dt>
<dd>In the North-West Atlantic landings have decreased from 4.2 million tons in the early 1970s to 1.9 million tons in 2013. During the 21st century some species have shown weak signs of recovery, including <a href="/wiki/Greenland_halibut" title="Greenland halibut">Greenland halibut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellowtail_flounder" title="Yellowtail flounder">yellowtail flounder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_halibut" title="Atlantic halibut">Atlantic halibut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haddock" title="Haddock">haddock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spiny_dogfish" title="Spiny dogfish">spiny dogfish</a>, while other stocks shown no such signs, including cod, <a href="/wiki/Witch_(righteye_flounder)" title="Witch (righteye flounder)">witch flounder</a>, and redfish. Stocks of invertebrates, in contrast, remain at record levels of abundance. 31% of stocks are overfished in the North-west Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOA-2016-p39_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-NOA-2016-p39-88">[88]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Time_series_for_collapse_of_Atlantic_northwest_cod.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="837" data-file-width="1284" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Time_series_for_collapse_of_Atlantic_northwest_cod.png/220px-Time_series_for_collapse_of_Atlantic_northwest_cod.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Time_series_for_collapse_of_Atlantic_northwest_cod.png/330px-Time_series_for_collapse_of_Atlantic_northwest_cod.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Time_series_for_collapse_of_Atlantic_northwest_cod.png/440px-Time_series_for_collapse_of_Atlantic_northwest_cod.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Time_series_for_collapse_of_Atlantic_northwest_cod.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Capture of Atlantic north-west cod in million tons</div></div></div>
<p>In 1497, <a href="/wiki/John_Cabot" title="John Cabot">John Cabot</a> became the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_European" title="Western European">Western European</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> to explore mainland North America and one of his major discoveries was the abundant resources of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">Atlantic cod</a> off <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newfoundland" title="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</a>. Referred to as "Newfoundland Currency" this discovery yielded some 200 million tons of fish over five centuries. In the late 19th and early 20th centuries new fisheries started to exploit <a href="/wiki/Haddock" title="Haddock">haddock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mackerel" title="Mackerel">mackerel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">lobster</a>. From the 1950s to the 1970s the introduction of European and Asian distant-water fleets in the area dramatically increased the fishing capacity and the number of exploited species. It also expanded the exploited areas from near-shore to the open sea and to great depths to include deep-water species such as <a href="/wiki/Sebastes" title="Sebastes">redfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland_halibut" title="Greenland halibut">Greenland halibut</a>, witch flounder, and <a href="/wiki/Grenadiers_(fish)" title="Grenadiers (fish)">grenadiers</a>. <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">Overfishing</a> in the area was recognised as early as the 1960s but, because this was occurring on <a href="/wiki/International_waters" title="International waters">international waters</a>, it took until the late 1970s before any attempts to regulate was made. In the early 1990s, this finally resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Atlantic_northwest_cod_fishery" title="Collapse of the Atlantic northwest cod fishery">collapse of the Atlantic northwest cod fishery</a>. The population of a number of deep-sea fishes also collapsed in the process, including <a href="/wiki/American_plaice" title="American plaice">American plaice</a>, redfish, and Greenland halibut, together with flounder and grenadier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Eastern Central Atlantic</dt>
<dd>In the Eastern Central Atlantic small <a href="/wiki/Pelagic_fish" title="Pelagic fish">pelagic fishes</a> constitute about 50% of landings with sardine reaching 0.6–1.0 million tons per year. Pelagic fish stocks are considered fully fished or overfished, with sardines south of <a href="/wiki/Cape_Bojador" title="Cape Bojador">Cape Bojador</a> the notable exception. Almost half of the stocks are fished at biologically unsustainable levels. Total catches have been fluctuating since the 1970s; reaching 3.9 million tons in 2013 or slightly less than the peak production in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOA-2016-p39_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-NOA-2016-p39-88">[88]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bahamas.A2001138.1550.250m.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Bahamas.A2001138.1550.250m.jpg/170px-Bahamas.A2001138.1550.250m.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Bahamas.A2001138.1550.250m.jpg/255px-Bahamas.A2001138.1550.250m.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Bahamas.A2001138.1550.250m.jpg/340px-Bahamas.A2001138.1550.250m.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bahamas.A2001138.1550.250m.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bahama_Banks" title="Bahama Banks">Bahama Banks</a></div></div></div>
<dl><dt>Western Central Atlantic</dt>
<dd>In the Western Central Atlantic, catches have been decreasing since 2000 and reached 1.3 million tons in 2013. The most important species in the area, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_menhaden" title="Gulf menhaden">Gulf menhaden</a>, reached a million tons in the mid-1980s but only half a million tons in 2013 and is now considered fully fished. <a href="/wiki/Round_sardinella" title="Round sardinella">Round sardinella</a> was an important species in the 1990s but is now considered overfished. <a href="/wiki/Grouper" title="Grouper">Groupers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lutjanidae" title="Lutjanidae">snappers</a> are overfished and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_brown_shrimp" title="Northern brown shrimp">northern brown shrimp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_oyster" title="Eastern oyster">American cupped oyster</a> are considered fully fished approaching overfished. 44% of stocks are being fished at unsustainable levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOA-2016-p39_88-4"><a href="#cite_note-NOA-2016-p39-88">[88]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agulhas_Bank_NOAA_ETOPO1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="417" data-file-width="703" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Agulhas_Bank_NOAA_ETOPO1.jpg/220px-Agulhas_Bank_NOAA_ETOPO1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Agulhas_Bank_NOAA_ETOPO1.jpg/330px-Agulhas_Bank_NOAA_ETOPO1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Agulhas_Bank_NOAA_ETOPO1.jpg/440px-Agulhas_Bank_NOAA_ETOPO1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agulhas_Bank_NOAA_ETOPO1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Bank" title="Agulhas Bank">Agulhas Bank</a></div></div></div>
<dl><dt>South-East Atlantic</dt>
<dd>In the South-East Atlantic catches have decreased from 3.3 million tons in the early 1970s to 1.3 million tons in 2013. <a href="/wiki/Horse_mackerel" title="Horse mackerel">Horse mackerel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hake" title="Hake">hake</a> are the most important species, together representing almost half of the landings. Off South Africa and Namibia <a href="/wiki/Merluccius_paradoxus" title="Merluccius paradoxus">deep-water hake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merluccius_capensis" title="Merluccius capensis">shallow-water Cape hake</a> have recovered to sustainable levels since regulations were introduced in 2006 and the states of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_African_pilchard" title="Southern African pilchard">Southern African pilchard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_African_anchovy" title="Southern African anchovy">anchovy</a> have improved to fully fished in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOA-2016-p39_88-5"><a href="#cite_note-NOA-2016-p39-88">[88]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt>South-West Atlantic</dt>
<dd>In the South-West Atlantic, a peak was reached in the mid-1980s and catches now fluctuate between 1.7 and 2.6 million tons. The most important species, the <a href="/wiki/Illex_argentinus" title="Illex argentinus">Argentine shortfin squid</a>, which reached half a million tons in 2013 or half the peak value, is considered fully fished to overfished. Another important species was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brazilian_sardinella" title="Brazilian sardinella">Brazilian sardinella</a>, with a production of 100,000 tons in 2013 it is now considered overfished. Half the stocks in this area are being fished at unsustainable levels: <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Whitehead%27s_round_herring&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Whitehead's round herring (page does not exist)">Whitehead's round herring</a> has not yet reached fully fished but <a href="/wiki/Cunene_horse_mackerel" title="Cunene horse mackerel">Cunene horse mackerel</a> is overfished. The sea snail <a href="/wiki/Haliotis_midae" title="Haliotis midae">perlemoen abalone</a> is targeted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illegal_fishing" title="Illegal fishing">illegal fishing</a> and remain overfished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOA-2016-p39_88-6"><a href="#cite_note-NOA-2016-p39-88">[88]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p><br/>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ocean_fisheries#Atlantic_Ocean" title="Ocean fisheries">Ocean fisheries § Atlantic Ocean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fishing_down_the_food_web" title="Fishing down the food web">Fishing down the food web</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Environmental issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nesocichla_eremita_-Inaccessible_Island,_British_overseas_territory-8_(3).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2543" data-file-width="3814" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Nesocichla_eremita_-Inaccessible_Island%2C_British_overseas_territory-8_%283%29.jpg/220px-Nesocichla_eremita_-Inaccessible_Island%2C_British_overseas_territory-8_%283%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Nesocichla_eremita_-Inaccessible_Island%2C_British_overseas_territory-8_%283%29.jpg/330px-Nesocichla_eremita_-Inaccessible_Island%2C_British_overseas_territory-8_%283%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Nesocichla_eremita_-Inaccessible_Island%2C_British_overseas_territory-8_%283%29.jpg/440px-Nesocichla_eremita_-Inaccessible_Island%2C_British_overseas_territory-8_%283%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nesocichla_eremita_-Inaccessible_Island,_British_overseas_territory-8_(3).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marine_debris" title="Marine debris">Marine debris</a> strewn over the beaches of the South Atlantic <a href="/wiki/Inaccessible_Island" title="Inaccessible Island">Inaccessible Island</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Endangered_species">Endangered species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Endangered species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44"
