<p>Endangered marine species include the <a href="/wiki/Manatee" title="Manatee">manatee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a>, sea lions, turtles, and whales. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drift_net" title="Drift net">Drift net</a> fishing can kill dolphins, <a href="/wiki/Albatross" title="Albatross">albatrosses</a> and other seabirds (<a href="/wiki/Petrel" title="Petrel">petrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Auk" title="Auk">auks</a>), hastening the fish stock decline and contributing to international disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_and_pollution">Waste and pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Waste and pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">Marine pollution</a> is a generic term for the entry into the ocean of potentially hazardous chemicals or particles. The biggest culprits are rivers and with them many agriculture <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> chemicals as well as livestock and human waste. The excess of oxygen-depleting chemicals leads to <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(environmental)" title="Hypoxia (environmental)">hypoxia</a> and the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Dead_zone_(ecology)" title="Dead zone (ecology)">dead zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Marine_debris" title="Marine debris">Marine debris</a>, which is also known as marine litter, describes human-created waste floating in a body of water. Oceanic debris tends to accumulate at the center of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oceanic_gyres" title="Oceanic gyres">gyres</a> and coastlines, frequently washing aground where it is known as beach litter. The <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_garbage_patch" title="North Atlantic garbage patch">North Atlantic garbage patch</a> is estimated to be hundreds of kilometers across in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other pollution concerns include agricultural and municipal waste. Municipal pollution comes from the eastern United States, southern Brazil, and eastern Argentina; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_pollution" title="Oil pollution">oil pollution</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Maracaibo" title="Lake Maracaibo">Lake Maracaibo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>; and industrial waste and municipal sewage pollution in the Baltic Sea, North Sea, and Mediterranean Sea.
</p><p>A USAF <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-124_Globemaster_II" title="C-124 Globemaster II">C-124</a> aircraft from <a href="/wiki/Dover_Air_Force_Base" title="Dover Air Force Base">Dover Air Force Base</a>, Delaware was carrying three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_bombs" title="Nuclear bombs">nuclear bombs</a> over the Atlantic Ocean when it experienced a loss of power. For their own safety, the crew jettisoned two nuclear bombs, which were never recovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shutdown_of_thermohaline_circulation" title="Shutdown of thermohaline circulation">Shutdown of thermohaline circulation</a></div>
<p>North Atlantic hurricane activity has increased over past decades because of increased sea surface temperature (SST) at tropical latitudes, changes that can be attributed to either the natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_Multidecadal_Oscillation" title="Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation">Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation</a> (AMO) or to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropogenic_climate_change" title="Anthropogenic climate change">anthropogenic climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mann_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mann-94">[94]</a></sup>
A 2005 report indicated that the Atlantic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meridional_overturning_circulation" title="Meridional overturning circulation">meridional overturning circulation</a> (AMOC) slowed down by 30% between 1957 and 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> If the AMO were responsible for SST variability, the AMOC would have increased in strength, which is apparently not the case. Furthermore, it is clear from statistical analyses of annual tropical cyclones that these changes do not display multidecadal cyclicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mann_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mann-94">[94]</a></sup> Therefore, these changes in SST must be caused by human activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ocean <a href="/wiki/Mixed_layer" title="Mixed layer">mixed layer</a> plays an important role in heat storage over seasonal and decadal time-scales, whereas deeper layers are affected over millennia and have a heat capacity about 50 times that of the mixed layer. This heat uptake provides a time-lag for climate change but it also results in thermal expansion of the oceans which contributes to <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a>. 21st-century global warming will probably result in an <a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_level" title="Equilibrium level">equilibrium sea-level</a> rise five times greater than today, whilst melting of glaciers, including that of the Greenland ice-sheet, expected to have virtually no effect during the 21st century, will probably result in a sea-level rise of 3–6 m over a millennium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
